## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: materials science – solid mechanics (thermoelasticity and temperature-dependent plasticity)
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which most naturally map to constitutive modeling of solids where elastic modulus, yield stress, and hardening/softening laws depend on temperature. Likely relationships include linear thermoelastic response at small strain and nonlinear/plastic flow with strain hardening combined with thermal softening at higher strain/temperature.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke’s law (isothermal linear elasticity): sigma = c0 * epsilon (small-strain elastic regime)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (linear): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic stiffness varies approximately linearly with temperature)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (exponential): sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic stiffness changes rapidly with temperature)
- Constrained thermoelasticity (thermal strain subtraction): sigma = c0 * (epsilon - c1 * (T - c2)) (stress from mechanical strain minus thermal expansion strain)
- Nonlinear elastic polynomial with thermal scaling: sigma = (c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon^2 + c2 * epsilon^3) * exp(c3 * T) (anharmonic/nonlinear elasticity with temperature effect)
- Hollomon power-law hardening with thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon^c1 * exp(c2 * T) (plastic flow stress increases with strain and decreases/increases with temperature via exp term)
- Ludwik hardening with temperature-dependent yield offset: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) + c2 * epsilon^c3 (yield stress shifts with temperature; hardening follows a power law)
- Voce saturation hardening with thermal scaling: sigma = (c0 - c1 * exp(c2 * epsilon)) * exp(c3 * T) (hardening saturates at large strain; saturation level varies with temperature)
- Exponential hardening/softening in strain with linear thermal softening: sigma = (c0 + c1 * (1 - exp(c2 * epsilon))) * (c3 + c4 * T) (rapid early hardening and a temperature-softening prefactor)
- Johnson–Cook-like multiplicative decomposition (strain term × thermal term): sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon^c2) * (c3 + c4 * T)^c5 (common engineering form for plastic flow with thermal softening/hardening)
- Zerilli–Armstrong-like additive thermal term: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon^c2 + c3 * exp(c4 * T) (separates athermal strengthening, strain hardening, and temperature-dependent softening)
- Smooth elastic–plastic transition (regularized yield): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * log(1 + exp(c2 * (epsilon - c3))) * exp(c4 * T) (captures elastic slope then plastic flow beyond a yield strain, with temperature dependence)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Small-strain linearity: a strictly linear sigma–epsilon relation indicates purely elastic behavior (single slope = effective modulus).
- Thermal expansion coupling: appearance of (epsilon - c*(T - c)) suggests stress arises from mismatch between imposed strain and thermal strain (thermoelastic constraint).
- Temperature-softening via multiplicative prefactor: sigma multiplied by a decreasing function of T (often linear in T or exp(c*T)) indicates reduced strength/stiffness at higher temperature.
- Strain hardening signature: sigma containing epsilon^n (n>0) indicates work hardening; n controls curvature (concave-down for 0<n<1, steeper for n>1).
- Hardening saturation: terms like (c0 - c1 * exp(c * epsilon)) imply sigma approaches a limiting stress at large strain (dynamic recovery/recrystallization-like saturation).
- Athermal + thermal decomposition: additive split sigma = f(epsilon) + g(T) suggests separate mechanisms (e.g., athermal obstacles plus thermally activated contribution).
- Yield/transition modeling: use of smooth switch functions (e.g., log(1+exp(...))) indicates an elastic-to-plastic transition around a characteristic strain c3.
- Modulus temperature dependence: sigma = E(T)*epsilon with E(T) linear/exponential in T often reflects lattice softening or phase-related stiffness changes.
- Nonlinear elastic terms: inclusion of epsilon^2, epsilon^3 terms without saturation typically indicates finite-strain or nonlinear elasticity effects rather than plasticity.

T appears inside cos(T+phase) and as 1/T inside exponentials, creating strong sensitivity near T→0; epsilon appears as |epsilon|, |epsilon|^3, and inside exp(c8|epsilon|+c9/T). The last term is extremely stiff: c9/T with c9≈2608 makes the inner exp enormous for small/moderate T, while c7=-1.522 then yields exp(negative huge)≈0, so the whole double-exponential typically collapses to ~c6*0≈0 (or becomes numerically delicate). Effective simplification (away from very large T): 7.77|epsilon|^3 − 3.81 cos(T+1.592)|epsilon| + 55.12 exp(-6.313/T)|epsilon|^0.266, with the double-exponential essentially degenerated to ~0.

Key structural requirements:
1) a pure \(\epsilon^3\) term (no \(T\) dependence)  
2) a term of the form \(\epsilon^{0.266369...}\exp(\text{const}/T)\) with **negative** const in numerator (i.e., \(\exp(-K/T)\))  
3) a term of the form \(\epsilon\sin(T-\text{const})\) (equivalently \(\epsilon\cos(T+\text{const})\) via phase shift), with **no extra \(T\)-amplitude factor** and no \(\epsilon\) inside the trig argument.

Also, conditional equivalence allows \(|\epsilon|=\epsilon\) assuming \(\epsilon\ge 0\).

For the trigonometric term: `epsilon*sin(T - c7)` can be rewritten. Note that `sin(T - c7) = sin(T)cos(c7) - cos(T)sin(c7)`. Also, `cos(T + c) = cos(T)cos(c) - sin(T)sin(c)`. Actually, `sin(x) = cos(x - π/2)`, so `sin(T - c7) = cos(T - c7 - π/2)`. And `cos(T + c2) = cos(T + c2)`. These match when `c2 = -c7 - π/2`. For the exponential term: `exp(-c4/(c5*T))` = `exp(constant/T)` where the constant is negative. So `exp(c/T)` with c being a free constant can match this.
